			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Recycling</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." /><img id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" class="fr-toggle-arrow" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;" alt="show/hide details" /></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" class="flaggedrevs_short_details" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Recycling">reviewed</a> on <i>14 March 2017</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Recycling_symbol.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Recycling_symbol.svg/220px-Recycling_symbol.svg.png" width="220" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Recycling_symbol.svg/330px-Recycling_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Recycling_symbol.svg/440px-Recycling_symbol.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="628" data-file-height="614" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Recycling_symbol.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The three chasing arrows of the international recycling logo. It is sometimes accompanied by the text "reduce, reuse, and recycle".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Recycling</b> is the process of converting <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">waste</a> materials into new materials and objects. It is an alternative to "conventional" waste disposal that can save material and help lower <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions (compared to plastic production,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup> for example). Recycling can prevent the waste of potentially useful materials and reduce the consumption of fresh raw materials, thereby reducing: <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> usage, air pollution (from <a href="/wiki/Incineration" title="Incineration">incineration</a>), and water pollution (from <a href="/wiki/Landfilling" class="mw-redirect" title="Landfilling">landfilling</a>).</p>
<p>Recycling is a key component of modern waste reduction and is the third component of the "<a href="/wiki/Waste_minimisation" title="Waste minimisation">Reduce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reuse" title="Reuse">Reuse</a>, and Recycle" <a href="/wiki/Waste_hierarchy" title="Waste hierarchy">waste hierarchy</a>.</p>
<p>There are some <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">ISO</a> standards related to recycling such as ISO 15270:2008 for plastics waste and <a href="/wiki/ISO_14001" class="mw-redirect" title="ISO 14001">ISO 14001</a>:2004 for environmental management control of recycling practice.</p>
<p>Recyclable materials include many kinds of glass, paper, and cardboard, metal, plastic, <a href="/wiki/Tire" title="Tire">tires</a>, textiles, and <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Composting" class="mw-redirect" title="Composting">composting</a> or other reuse of <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_waste" title="Biodegradable waste">biodegradable waste</a>—such as <a href="/wiki/Food_waste" title="Food waste">food</a> or <a href="/wiki/Green_waste" title="Green waste">garden waste</a>—is also considered recycling.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup> Materials to be recycled are either brought to a collection centre or picked up from the curbside, then sorted, cleaned, and reprocessed into new materials destined for manufacturing.</p>
<p>In the strictest sense, recycling of a material would produce a fresh supply of the same material—for example, used office paper would be converted into new office paper or used <a href="/wiki/Polystyrene" title="Polystyrene">polystyrene</a> foam into new polystyrene. However, this is often difficult or too expensive (compared with producing the same product from raw materials or other sources), so "recycling" of many products or materials involves their <i><a href="/wiki/Reuse" title="Reuse">reuse</a></i> in producing different materials (for example, <a href="/wiki/Paperboard" title="Paperboard">paperboard</a>) instead. Another form of recycling is the <a href="/wiki/Waste_sorting" title="Waste sorting">salvage</a> of certain materials from complex products, either due to their intrinsic value (such as lead from <a href="/wiki/Car_batteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Car batteries">car batteries</a>, or gold from <a href="/wiki/Circuit_board" class="mw-redirect" title="Circuit board">circuit boards</a>), or due to their hazardous nature (e.g., removal and reuse of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> from <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermostat" title="Thermostat">thermostats</a>).</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Recycling has been a common practice for most of human history, with recorded advocates as far back as <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> in 400&#160;BC. During periods when resources were scarce and hard to come by, archaeological studies of ancient waste dumps show less household waste (such as ash, broken tools, and pottery)—implying more waste was being recycled in the absence of new material.<sup id="cite_ref-guide_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guide-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg/220px-Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg/330px-Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg/440px-Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1999" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An American poster from <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Pre-industrial" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-industrial">pre-industrial</a> times, there is evidence of scrap bronze and other metals being collected in Europe and melted down for perpetual reuse.<sup id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">[4]</a></sup> Paper recycling was first recorded in 1031 when Japanese shops sold repulped paper.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> In Britain dust and ash from wood and coal fires was collected by "<a href="/wiki/Waste_collector" title="Waste collector">dustmen</a>" and <a href="/wiki/Downcycling" title="Downcycling">downcycled</a> as a base material used in brick making. The main driver for these types of recycling was the economic advantage of obtaining recycled feedstock instead of acquiring virgin material, as well as a lack of public waste removal in ever more densely populated areas.<sup id="cite_ref-guide_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guide-3">[3]</a></sup> In 1813, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Law_(inventor)" title="Benjamin Law (inventor)">Benjamin Law</a> developed the process of turning rags into "<a href="/wiki/Shoddy" class="mw-redirect" title="Shoddy">shoddy</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_textile_manufacturing#M" title="Glossary of textile manufacturing">mungo</a>" wool in Batley, Yorkshire. This material combined recycled fibers with virgin <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>. The <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a> shoddy industry in towns such as Batley and Dewsbury lasted from the early 19th century to at least 1914.</p>
<p>Industrialization spurred demand for affordable materials; aside from rags, ferrous <a href="/wiki/Scrap" title="Scrap">scrap</a> metals were coveted as they were cheaper to acquire than virgin ore. Railroads both purchased and sold scrap metal in the 19th century, and the growing steel and automobile industries purchased scrap in the early 20th century. Many secondary goods were collected, processed and sold by peddlers who scoured dumps and city streets for discarded machinery, pots, pans, and other sources of metal. By <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, thousands of such peddlers roamed the streets of American cities, taking advantage of market forces to recycle post-consumer materials back into industrial production.<sup id="cite_ref-cash_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cash-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beverage bottles were recycled with a refundable deposit at some drink manufacturers in Great Britain and Ireland around 1800, notably <a href="/wiki/Schweppes" title="Schweppes">Schweppes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> An official recycling system with refundable <a href="/wiki/Container_deposit_legislation" title="Container deposit legislation">deposits</a> was established in Sweden for bottles in 1884 and aluminum beverage cans in 1982; the law led to a recycling rate for beverage containers of 84–99 percent depending on type, and a glass bottle can be refilled over 20 times on average.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper,_rags,_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg/220px-INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg/330px-INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg/440px-INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2492" data-file-height="3520" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper,_rags,_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
British poster from World War II</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wartime">Wartime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Wartime">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>New chemical industries created in the late 19th century both invented new materials (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bakelite" title="Bakelite">Bakelite</a> (1907) and promised to transform valueless into valuable materials. Proverbially, you could not <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/make_a_silk_purse_of_a_sow%27s_ear" class="extiw" title="wikt:make a silk purse of a sow's ear">make a silk purse of a sow's ear</a>—until the US firm Arhur D. Little published in 1921 "On the Making of Silk Purses from Sows' Ears", its research proving that when "chemistry puts on overalls and gets down to business . . .new values appear. New and better paths are opened to reach the goals desired."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recycling was a highlight throughout World War II. During the war, financial constraints and significant material shortages due to war efforts made it necessary for countries to reuse goods and recycle materials.<sup id="cite_ref-PBS_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-10">[10]</a></sup> These resource shortages caused by the <a href="/wiki/World_war" title="World war">world wars</a>, and other such world-changing occurrences, greatly encouraged recycling.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The struggles of war claimed much of the material resources available, leaving little for the civilian population.<sup id="cite_ref-PBS_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-10">[10]</a></sup> It became necessary for most homes to recycle their waste, as recycling offered an extra source of materials allowing people to make the most of what was available to them. Recycling household materials meant more resources for war efforts and a better chance of victory.<sup id="cite_ref-PBS_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBS-10">[10]</a></sup> Massive government promotion campaigns were carried out in the <a href="/wiki/Home_front_during_World_War_II" title="Home front during World War II">home front during World War II</a> in every country involved in the war, urging citizens to donate metals and conserve fiber, as a matter of patriotism.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Post-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A considerable investment in recycling occurred in the 1970s, due to rising energy costs.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Recycling aluminum uses only 5% of the energy required by virgin production; glass, paper and other metals have less dramatic but very significant energy savings when recycled feedstock is used.<sup id="cite_ref-economistrecycle_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economistrecycle-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although consumer electronics such as the television have been popular since the 1920s, recycling of them was almost unheard of until early 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The first electronic waste recycling scheme was implemented in Switzerland, beginning with collection of old refrigerators but gradually expanding to cover all devices.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> After these schemes were set up, many countries did not have the capacity to deal with the sheer quantity of e-waste they generated or its hazardous nature. They began to export the problem to developing countries without enforced environmental legislation. This is cheaper, as recycling computer monitors in the United States costs 10 times more than in China. Demand in Asia for electronic waste began to grow when scrap yards found that they could extract valuable substances such as copper, silver, iron, silicon, nickel, and gold, during the recycling process.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The 2000s saw a large increase in both the sale of electronic devices and their growth as a waste stream: in 2002, e-waste grew faster than any other type of waste in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-17">[17]</a></sup> This caused investment in modern, automated facilities to cope with the influx of redundant appliances, especially after strict laws were implemented in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2014, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> has about 50% of world share of the waste and recycling industries, with over 60,000 companies employing 500,000 persons, with a turnover of €24 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Countries have to reach recycling rates of at least 50%, while the lead countries are around 65% and the EU average is 39% as of 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation">Legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supply">Supply</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Supply">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For a recycling program to work, having a large, stable <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply</a> of recyclable material is crucial. Three legislative options have been used to create such a supply: mandatory recycling collection, <a href="/wiki/Container_deposit_legislation" title="Container deposit legislation">container deposit legislation</a>, and refuse bans. Mandatory collection laws set recycling targets for cities to aim for, usually in the form that a certain percentage of a material must be diverted from the city's waste stream by a target date. The city is then responsible for working to meet this target.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Container deposit legislation involves offering a refund for the return of certain containers, typically glass, plastic, and metal. When a product in such a container is purchased, a small surcharge is added to the price. This surcharge can be reclaimed by the consumer if the container is returned to a collection point. These programs have been very successful, often resulting in an 80 percent recycling rate.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Despite such good results, the shift in collection costs from local government to industry and consumers has created strong opposition to the creation of such programs in some areas.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup> A variation on this is where the manufacturer bears responsibility for the recycling of their goods. In the European Union, the WEEE Directive requires producers of consumer electronics to reimburse the recyclers' costs.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>An alternative way to increase supply of recyclates is to <a href="/wiki/Ban_(law)" title="Ban (law)">ban</a> the disposal of certain materials as waste, often including used oil, old batteries, tires, and garden waste. One aim of this method is to create a viable economy for proper disposal of banned products. Care must be taken that enough of these recycling <a href="/wiki/Service_(economics)" title="Service (economics)">services</a> exist, or such bans simply lead to increased <a href="/wiki/Fly-tipping" class="mw-redirect" title="Fly-tipping">illegal dumping</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government-mandated_demand">Government-mandated demand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Government-mandated demand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Legislation has also been used to increase and maintain a demand for recycled materials. Four methods of such legislation exist: minimum recycled content mandates, utilization rates, <a href="/wiki/Procurement" title="Procurement">procurement</a> policies, and recycled <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_labeling" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandatory labeling">product labeling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both minimum recycled content mandates and utilization rates increase demand directly by forcing manufacturers to include recycling in their operations. Content mandates specify that a certain percentage of a new product must consist of recycled material. Utilization rates are a more flexible option: industries are permitted to meet the recycling targets at any point of their operation or even contract recycling out in exchange for tradeable credits. Opponents to both of these methods point to the large increase in reporting requirements they impose, and claim that they rob industry of necessary flexibility.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DeLong_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeLong-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Governments" class="mw-redirect" title="Governments">Governments</a> have used their own <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power" title="Purchasing power">purchasing power</a> to increase recycling demand through what are called "procurement policies." These policies are either "set-asides," which reserve a certain amount of spending solely towards recycled products, or "price preference" programs which provide a larger <a href="/wiki/Government_budget" title="Government budget">budget</a> when recycled items are purchased. Additional regulations can target specific cases: in the United States, for example, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> mandates the purchase of oil, paper, tires and building insulation from recycled or re-refined sources whenever possible.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The final government regulation towards increased demand is recycled product labeling. When producers are required to label their packaging with amount of recycled material in the product (including the packaging), consumers are better able to make educated choices. Consumers with sufficient <a href="/wiki/Buying_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Buying power">buying power</a> can then choose more environmentally conscious options, prompt producers to increase the amount of recycled material in their products, and indirectly increase demand. Standardized recycling labeling can also have a positive effect on supply of recyclates if the labeling includes information on how and where the product can be recycled.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recyclates">Recyclates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Recyclates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg/220px-CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg/330px-CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg/440px-CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2035" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Glass recovered by crushing only one kind of beer bottle</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Recyclate</b> is a raw material that is sent to, and processed in a waste recycling plant or materials recovery facility which will be used to form new products.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The material is collected in various methods and delivered to a facility where it undergoes re-manufacturing so that it can used in the production of new materials or products. For example, <a href="/wiki/Plastic_bottle" title="Plastic bottle">plastic bottles</a> that are collected can be re-used and made into plastic pellets, a new product.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality_of_recyclate">Quality of recyclate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Quality of recyclate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The quality of recyclates is recognized as one of the principal challenges that needs to be addressed for the success of a long-term vision of a green economy and achieving zero waste. Recyclate quality is generally referring to how much of the raw material is made up of target material compared to the amount of non-target material and other non-recyclable material.<sup id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">[29]</a></sup> Only target material is likely to be recycled, so a higher amount of non-target and non-recyclable material will reduce the quantity of recycling product.<sup id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">[29]</a></sup> A high proportion of non-target and non-recyclable material can make it more difficult for re-processors to achieve "high-quality" recycling. If the recyclate is of poor quality, it is more likely to end up being down-cycled or, in more extreme cases, sent to other recovery options or landfilled.<sup id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">[29]</a></sup> For example, to facilitate the re-manufacturing of clear glass products there are tight restrictions for colored glass going into the re-melt process.</p>
<p>The quality of recyclate not only supports high-quality recycling, but it can also deliver significant environmental benefits by reducing, reusing and keeping products out of landfills.<sup id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">[29]</a></sup> High-quality recycling can help support growth in the economy by maximizing the economic value of the waste material collected.<sup id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">[29]</a></sup> Higher income levels from the sale of quality recyclates can return value which can be significant to local governments, households, and businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">[29]</a></sup> Pursuing high-quality recycling can also provide consumer and business confidence in the waste and resource management sector and may encourage investment in that sector.</p>
<p>There are many actions along the recycling supply chain that can influence and affect the material quality of recyclate.<sup id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">[30]</a></sup> It begins with the waste producers who place non-target and non-recyclable wastes in recycling collection. This can affect the quality of final recyclate streams or require further efforts to discard those materials at later stages in the recycling process.<sup id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">[30]</a></sup> The different collection systems can result in different levels of contamination. Depending on which materials are collected together, extra effort is required to sort this material back into separate streams and can significantly reduce the quality of the final product.<sup id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">[30]</a></sup> Transportation and the compaction of materials can make it more difficult to separate material back into separate waste streams. Sorting facilities are not one hundred per cent effective in separating materials, despite improvements in technology and quality recyclate which can see a loss in recyclate quality.<sup id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">[30]</a></sup> The storage of materials outside where the product can become wet can cause problems for re-processors. Reprocessing facilities may require further sorting steps to further reduce the amount of non-target and non-recyclable material.<sup id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">[30]</a></sup> Each action along the recycling path plays a part in the quality of recyclate.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quality_recyclate_action_plan_.28Scotland.29">Quality recyclate action plan (Scotland)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Quality recyclate action plan (Scotland)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Recyclate Quality Action Plan of Scotland sets out a number of proposed actions that the Scottish Government would like to take forward in order to drive up the quality of the materials being collected for recycling and sorted at materials recovery facilities before being exported or sold on to the reprocessing market.<sup id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The plan's objectives are to:<sup id="cite_ref-highland_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-highland-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Drive up the quality of recyclate.</li>
<li>Deliver greater transparency about the quality of recyclate.</li>
<li>Provide help to those contracting with materials recycling facilities to identify what is required of them</li>
<li>Ensure compliance with the Waste (Scotland) regulations 2012.</li>
<li>Stimulate a household market for quality recyclate.</li>
<li>Address and reduce issues surrounding the Waste Shipment Regulations.</li>
</ul>
<p>The plan focuses on three key areas, with fourteen actions which were identified to increase the quality of materials collected, sorted and presented to the processing market in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-highland_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-highland-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The three areas of focus are:<sup id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>Collection systems and input contamination</li>
<li>Sorting facilities – material sampling and transparency</li>
<li>Material quality benchmarking and standards</li>
</ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling_consumer_waste">Recycling consumer waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Recycling consumer waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collection">Collection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Collection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg/220px-DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg/330px-DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg/440px-DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg 2x" data-file-width="644" data-file-height="967" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A three-sided bin at a railway station in Germany, intended to separate paper <i>(left)</i> and plastic wrappings <i>(right)</i> from other waste <i>(back)</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A number of different systems have been implemented to collect recyclates from the general waste stream. These systems lie along the spectrum of trade-off between public convenience and government ease and expense. The three main categories of collection are "drop-off centers," "buy-back centers", and "curbside collection."<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Curbside_collection">Curbside collection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Curbside collection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Curbside_collection" class="mw-redirect" title="Curbside collection">Curbside collection</a></div>
<p>Curbside collection encompasses many subtly different systems, which differ mostly on where in the process the recyclates are sorted and cleaned. The main categories are mixed waste collection, commingled recyclables, and source separation.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Waste_collection_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste collection vehicle">waste collection vehicle</a> generally picks up the waste.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ACT_recycling_truck.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ACT_recycling_truck.jpg/220px-ACT_recycling_truck.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ACT_recycling_truck.jpg/330px-ACT_recycling_truck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ACT_recycling_truck.jpg/440px-ACT_recycling_truck.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ACT_recycling_truck.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A recycling truck collecting the contents of a <a href="/wiki/Recycling_bin" title="Recycling bin">recycling bin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">Canberra</a>, Australia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At one end of the spectrum is mixed waste collection, in which all recyclates are collected mixed in with the rest of the waste, and the desired material is then sorted out and cleaned at a central sorting facility. This results in a large amount of recyclable waste, paper especially, being too soiled to reprocess, but has advantages as well: the city need not pay for a separate collection of recyclates and no public education is needed. Any changes to which materials are recyclable is easy to accommodate as all sorting happens in a central location.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a commingled or <a href="/wiki/Single-stream_recycling" title="Single-stream recycling">single-stream system</a>, all recyclables for collection are mixed but kept separate from other waste. This greatly reduces the need for post-collection cleaning but does require <a href="/wiki/Public_education" class="mw-redirect" title="Public education">public education</a> on what materials are recyclable.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Source separation is the other extreme, where each material is cleaned and sorted prior to collection. This method requires the least post-collection sorting and produces the purest recyclates, but incurs additional <a href="/wiki/Operating_cost" title="Operating cost">operating costs</a> for collection of each separate material. An extensive public education program is also required, which must be successful if recyclate contamination is to be avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Source separation used to be the preferred method due to the high sorting costs incurred by commingled (mixed waste) collection. Advances in sorting technology (see <a href="/wiki/Recycling#Sorting" title="Recycling">sorting</a> below), however, have lowered this overhead substantially—many areas which had developed source separation programs have since switched to co-mingled collection.<sup id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Buy-back_centers">Buy-back centers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Buy-back centers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Buy-back centers differ in that the cleaned recyclates are purchased, thus providing a clear incentive for use and creating a stable supply. The post-processed material can then be sold on, hopefully creating a profit. Unfortunately, government subsidies are necessary to make buy-back centres a viable enterprise, as according to the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Waste_%26_Recycling_Association" title="National Waste &amp; Recycling Association">National Waste &amp; Recycling Association</a>, it costs on average US$50 to process a ton of material, which can only be resold for US$30.<sup id="cite_ref-gar_2-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gar-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Drop-off_centers">Drop-off centers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Drop-off centers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Drop-off centers require the waste producer to carry the recyclates to a central location, either an installed or mobile collection station or the reprocessing plant itself. They are the easiest type of collection to establish but suffer from low and unpredictable throughput.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Distributed_Recycling">Distributed Recycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Distributed Recycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For some waste materials such as plastic, recent technical devices called <a href="/wiki/Recyclebots" class="mw-redirect" title="Recyclebots">recyclebots</a><sup id="cite_ref-RPJ_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RPJ-32">[32]</a></sup> enable a form of distributed recycling. Preliminary <a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_analysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Life-cycle analysis">life-cycle analysis</a> (LCA) indicates that such distributed recycling of <a href="/wiki/HDPE" class="mw-redirect" title="HDPE">HDPE</a> to make filament of <a href="/wiki/3-D_printers" class="mw-redirect" title="3-D printers">3-D printers</a> in rural regions is energetically favorable to either using virgin resin or conventional recycling processes because of reductions in transportation energy.<sup id="cite_ref-LCA_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LCA-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sorting">Sorting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sorting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:854px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Recycling_Video.webm/854px--Recycling_Video.webm.jpg&quot; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Recycling_Video.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Recycling sorting facility and processes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Once commingled recyclates are collected and delivered to a <a href="/wiki/Materials_recovery_facility" title="Materials recovery facility">central collection facility</a>, the different types of materials must be sorted. This is done in a series of stages, many of which involve automated processes such that a truckload of material can be fully sorted in less than an hour.<sup id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">[4]</a></sup> Some plants can now sort the materials automatically, known as <a href="/wiki/Single-stream_recycling" title="Single-stream recycling">single-stream recycling</a>. In plants, a variety of materials is sorted such as paper, different types of plastics, glass, metals, food scraps, and most types of batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> A 30 percent increase in recycling rates has been seen in the areas where these plants exist.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially, the commingled recyclates are removed from the collection vehicle and placed on a conveyor belt spread out in a single layer. Large pieces of <a href="/wiki/Corrugated_fiberboard" title="Corrugated fiberboard">corrugated fiberboard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plastic_bag" title="Plastic bag">plastic bags</a> are removed by hand at this stage, as they can cause later machinery to jam.<sup id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG/220px-Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG/330px-Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG/440px-Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2195" data-file-height="1564" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Early sorting of recyclable materials: glass and plastic bottles in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Next, automated machinery such as disk screens and air classifiers separate the recyclates by weight, splitting lighter paper and plastic from heavier glass and metal. Cardboard is removed from the mixed paper and the most common types of plastic, <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">PET</a> (#1) and <a href="/wiki/HDPE" class="mw-redirect" title="HDPE">HDPE</a> (#2), are collected. This separation is usually done by hand but has become automated in some sorting centers: a <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopic</a> scanner is used to differentiate between different types of paper and plastic based on the absorbed wavelengths, and subsequently divert each material into the proper collection channel.<sup id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Strong magnets are used to separate out <a href="/wiki/Ferrous_metal" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferrous metal">ferrous metals</a>, such as iron, steel, and <a href="/wiki/Tin_can" title="Tin can">tin cans</a>. <a href="/wiki/Non-ferrous_metal" title="Non-ferrous metal">Non-ferrous metals</a> are ejected by magnetic <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">eddy currents</a> in which a rotating <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">induces</a> an electric current around the aluminum cans, which in turn creates a magnetic eddy current inside the cans. This magnetic <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">eddy current</a> is repulsed by a large magnetic field, and the cans are ejected from the rest of the recyclate stream.<sup id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg/220px-Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg/330px-Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg/440px-Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="379" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A recycling point in <a href="/wiki/New_Byth" title="New Byth">New Byth</a>, Scotland, with separate containers for paper, plastics, and differently colored glass</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Finally, glass is sorted according to its color: brown, amber, green, or clear. It may either be sorted by hand,<sup id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">[4]</a></sup> or via an automated machine that uses colored filters to detect different colors. Glass fragments smaller than 10 millimetres (0.39&#160;in) across cannot be sorted automatically, and are mixed together as "glass fines."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>This process of recycling as well as reusing the recycled material has proven advantageous because it reduces amount of waste sent to landfills, conserves natural resources, saves energy, reduces greenhouse gas emissions, and helps create new jobs. Recycled materials can also be converted into new products that can be consumed again, such as paper, plastic, and glass.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The City and County of San Francisco's Department of the Environment is attempting to achieve a citywide goal of generating zero waste by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> San Francisco's refuse hauler, <a href="/wiki/Recology" title="Recology">Recology</a>, operates an effective recyclables sorting facility in San Francisco, which helped San Francisco reach a record-breaking diversion rate of 80%.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rinsing">Rinsing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Rinsing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Food_packaging" title="Food packaging">Food packaging</a> should no longer contain any organic matter (organic matter, if any, needs to be placed in a <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_waste" title="Biodegradable waste">biodegradable waste bin</a> or be buried in a garden<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>). Since no trace of biodegradable material is best kept in the packaging before placing it in a <a href="/wiki/Trash_bag" class="mw-redirect" title="Trash bag">trash bag</a>, some packaging also needs to be rinsed.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling_industrial_waste">Recycling industrial waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Recycling industrial waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shredded_tires.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shredded_tires.JPG/220px-Shredded_tires.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shredded_tires.JPG/330px-Shredded_tires.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shredded_tires.JPG/440px-Shredded_tires.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shredded_tires.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mounds of shredded rubber tires are ready for processing</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although many government programs are concentrated on recycling at home, a 64% of waste in the United Kingdom is generated by industry.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The focus of many recycling programs done by industry is the cost–effectiveness of recycling. The ubiquitous nature of cardboard packaging makes cardboard a commonly recycled waste product by companies that deal heavily in packaged goods, like <a href="/wiki/Retail_store" class="mw-redirect" title="Retail store">retail stores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warehouse" title="Warehouse">warehouses</a>, and distributors of goods. Other industries deal in niche or specialized products, depending on the nature of the waste materials that are present.</p>
<p>The glass, lumber, <a href="/wiki/Wood_pulp" class="mw-redirect" title="Wood pulp">wood pulp</a> and paper manufacturers all deal directly in commonly recycled materials; however, old <a href="/wiki/Rubber_tires" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubber tires">rubber tires</a> may be collected and recycled by independent tire dealers for a profit.</p>
<p>Levels of metals recycling are generally low. In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>, hosted by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP) published reports on metal stocks that exist within society<sup id="cite_ref-IRP_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRP-44">[44]</a></sup> and their recycling rates.<sup id="cite_ref-IRP_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IRP-44">[44]</a></sup> The Panel reported that the increase in the use of metals during the 20th and into the 21st century has led to a substantial shift in metal stocks from below ground to use in applications within society above ground. For example, the in-use stock of copper in the USA grew from 73 to 238&#160;kg per capita between 1932 and 1999.</p>
<p>The report authors observed that, as metals are inherently recyclable, the metal stocks in society can serve as huge mines above ground (the term "<b>urban mining</b>" has been coined with this idea in mind<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>). However, they found that the recycling rates of many metals are very low. The report warned that the recycling rates of some rare metals used in applications such as mobile phones, battery packs for hybrid cars and fuel cells, are so low that unless future end-of-life recycling rates are dramatically stepped up these critical metals will become unavailable for use in modern technology.</p>
<p>The military recycles some metals. The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Navy">U.S. Navy</a>'s Ship Disposal Program uses <a href="/wiki/Ship_breaking" title="Ship breaking">ship breaking</a> to reclaim the steel of old vessels. Ships may also be sunk to create an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_reef" title="Artificial reef">artificial reef</a>. <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">Uranium</a> is a very dense metal that has qualities superior to lead and <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> for many military and industrial uses. The <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> left over from processing it into <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a> and fuel for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">depleted uranium</a>, and it is used by all branches of the U.S. military use for armour-piercing shells and shielding.</p>
<p>The construction industry may recycle concrete and old road surface pavement, selling their waste materials for profit.</p>
<p>Some industries, like the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> industry and <a href="/wiki/Solar_photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar photovoltaic">solar photovoltaic</a> technology, in particular, are being proactive in setting up recycling policies even before there is considerable volume to their waste streams, anticipating future demand during their rapid growth.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recycling of plastics is more difficult, as most programs are not able to reach the necessary level of quality. Recycling of <a href="/wiki/PVC" class="mw-redirect" title="PVC">PVC</a> often results in <a href="/wiki/Downcycling" title="Downcycling">downcycling</a> of the material, which means only products of lower quality standard can be made with the recycled material. A new approach which allows an equal level of quality is the <a href="/wiki/Vinyloop" title="Vinyloop">Vinyloop</a> process. It was used after the <a href="/wiki/London_Olympics_2012" class="mw-redirect" title="London Olympics 2012">London Olympics 2012</a> to fulfill the PVC Policy.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="E-waste_recycling">E-waste recycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: E-waste recycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Computer_recycling" title="Computer recycling">Computer recycling</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RetiredCPUs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RetiredCPUs.jpg/220px-RetiredCPUs.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RetiredCPUs.jpg/330px-RetiredCPUs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RetiredCPUs.jpg/440px-RetiredCPUs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2792" data-file-height="1828" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RetiredCPUs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Microprocessors retrieved from waste stream</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/E-waste" class="mw-redirect" title="E-waste">E-waste</a> is a growing problem, accounting for 20-50 million metric tons of global waste per year according to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">EPA</a>. It is also the fastest growing waste stream in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinver_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinver-17">[17]</a></sup> Many recyclers do not recycle e-waste responsibly. After the cargo barge <a href="/wiki/Khian_Sea_waste_disposal_incident" title="Khian Sea waste disposal incident">Khian Sea</a> dumped 14,000 metric tons of toxic ash in <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Basel_Convention" title="Basel Convention">Basel Convention</a> was formed to stem the flow of hazardous substances into poorer countries. They created the <a href="/wiki/E-Stewards" title="E-Stewards">e-Stewards</a> certification to ensure that recyclers are held to the highest standards for environmental responsibility and to help consumers identify responsible recyclers. This works alongside other prominent legislation, such as the <a href="/wiki/Waste_Electrical_and_Electronic_Equipment_Directive" title="Waste Electrical and Electronic Equipment Directive">Waste Electrical and Electronic Equipment Directive</a> of the EU the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> National Computer Recycling Act, to prevent poisonous chemicals from entering waterways and the atmosphere.</p>
<p>In the recycling process, television sets, monitors, cell phones, and computers are typically tested for reuse and repaired. If broken, they may be disassembled for parts still having high value if labor is cheap enough. Other e-waste is shredded to pieces roughly 10 centimetres (3.9&#160;in) in size, and manually checked to separate out toxic batteries and <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitors</a> which contain poisonous metals. The remaining pieces are further shredded to 10 millimetres (0.39&#160;in) particles and passed under a magnet to remove ferrous metals. An <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">eddy current</a> ejects non-ferrous metals, which are sorted by density either by a centrifuge or vibrating plates. Precious metals can be dissolved in acid, sorted, and smelted into ingots. The remaining glass and plastic fractions are separated by density and sold to re-processors. Television sets and monitors must be manually disassembled to remove lead from CRTs or the mercury backlight from LCDs.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plastic_recycling">Plastic recycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Plastic recycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plastic_recycling" title="Plastic recycling">Plastic recycling</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg/220px-Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg/330px-Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg/440px-Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A container for recycling used plastic spoons into material for 3D printing</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Plastic recycling is the process of recovering scrap or waste plastic and reprocessing the material into useful products, sometimes completely different in form from their original state. For instance, this could mean melting down soft drink bottles and then casting them as plastic chairs and tables.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_recycling">Physical recycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Physical recycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some plastics are remelted to form new plastic objects; for example, PET water bottles can be converted into polyester destined for clothing. A disadvantage of this type of recycling is that the molecular weight of the polymer can change further and the levels of unwanted substances in the plastic can increase with each remelt.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_recycling">Chemical recycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chemical recycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>For some polymers, it is possible to convert them back into monomers, for example, PET can be treated with an alcohol and a catalyst to form a dialkyl terephthalate. The terephthalate diester can be used with ethylene glycol to form a new polyester polymer, thus making it possible to use the pure polymer again.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_plastic_pyrolysis_to_fuel_oil">Waste plastic pyrolysis to fuel oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Waste plastic pyrolysis to fuel oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another process involves conversion of assorted polymers into petroleum by a much less precise thermal <a href="/wiki/Depolymerization" title="Depolymerization">depolymerization</a> process. Such a process would be able to accept almost any polymer or mix of polymers, including <a href="/wiki/Thermoset" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermoset">thermoset</a> materials such as vulcanized rubber tires and the <a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">biopolymers</a> in feathers and other agricultural waste. Like natural petroleum, the chemicals produced can be used as fuels or as feedstock. A RESEM Technology<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> plant of this type in <a href="/wiki/Carthage,_Missouri" title="Carthage, Missouri">Carthage, Missouri</a>, USA, uses turkey waste as input material. Gasification is a similar process but is not technically recycling since polymers are not likely to become the result. Plastic Pyrolysis can convert petroleum based waste streams such as plastics into quality fuels, carbons. Given below is the list of suitable plastic raw materials for <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a>:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mixed plastic (<a href="/wiki/HDPE" class="mw-redirect" title="HDPE">HDPE</a>, <a href="/wiki/LDPE" class="mw-redirect" title="LDPE">LDPE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">PE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polypropylene" title="Polypropylene">PP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">Nylon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teflon" class="mw-redirect" title="Teflon">Teflon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polystyrene" title="Polystyrene">PS</a>, <a href="/wiki/ABS_plastic" class="mw-redirect" title="ABS plastic">ABS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fibre-reinforced_plastic" title="Fibre-reinforced plastic">FRP</a>, etc.)</li>
<li>Mixed waste plastic from waste paper mill</li>
<li>Multi-layered plastic</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling_codes">Recycling codes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Recycling codes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recycling_codes" title="Recycling codes">Recycling codes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg/220px-Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg/330px-Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg/440px-Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2776" data-file-height="1875" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Recycling codes on products</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In order to meet recyclers' needs while providing manufacturers a consistent, uniform system, a <a href="/wiki/Recycling_codes" title="Recycling codes">coding system</a> was developed. The recycling code for plastics was introduced in 1988 by the plastics industry through the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Plastics_Industry" title="Society of the Plastics Industry">Society of the Plastics Industry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Because municipal recycling programs traditionally have targeted packaging—primarily bottles and containers—the <a href="/wiki/Resin_identification_code" title="Resin identification code">resin coding system</a> offered a means of identifying the resin content of bottles and containers commonly found in the residential waste stream.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plastic products are printed with numbers 1–7 depending on the type of resin. Type 1 (<a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">polyethylene terephthalate</a>) is commonly found in soft drink and water bottles. Type 2 (high-density polyethylene) is found in most hard plastics such as milk jugs, laundry detergent bottles, and some dishware. Type 3 (polyvinyl chloride) includes items such as shampoo bottles, shower curtains, <a href="/wiki/Hula_hoops" class="mw-redirect" title="Hula hoops">hula hoops</a>, <a href="/wiki/Credit_cards" class="mw-redirect" title="Credit cards">credit cards</a>, wire jacketing, medical equipment, siding, and piping. Type 4 (low-density polyethylene) is found in shopping bags, squeezable bottles, tote bags, clothing, furniture, and carpet. Type 5 is <a href="/wiki/Polypropylene" title="Polypropylene">polypropylene</a> and makes up syrup bottles, straws, <a href="/wiki/Tupperware" title="Tupperware">Tupperware</a>, and some automotive parts. Type 6 is <a href="/wiki/Polystyrene" title="Polystyrene">polystyrene</a> and makes up meat trays, egg cartons, clamshell containers, and compact disc cases. Type 7 includes all other plastics such as bulletproof materials, 3- and 5-gallon water bottles, and sunglasses.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Having a recycling code or the chasing arrows logo on a material is not an automatic indicator that a material is recyclable but rather an explanation of what the material is. Types 1 and 2 are the most commonly recycled.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">Economic impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Economic impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Critics dispute the net economic and environmental benefits of recycling over its costs, and suggest that proponents of recycling often make matters worse and suffer from <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_bias" title="Confirmation bias">confirmation bias</a>. Specifically, critics argue that the costs and energy used in collection and transportation detract from (and outweigh) the costs and energy saved in the production process; also that the jobs produced by the recycling industry can be a poor trade for the jobs lost in logging, mining, and other industries associated with production; and that materials such as paper pulp can only be recycled a few times before material degradation prevents further recycling.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Waste_and_Recycling_Association&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="National Waste and Recycling Association (page does not exist)">National Waste and Recycling Association</a> (NWRA), reported in May 2015, that recycling and waste made a $6.7 billion economic impact in Ohio, U.S., and employed 14,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost.E2.80.93benefit_analysis">Cost–benefit analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Cost–benefit analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; font-size:85%; margin-left:1em;">

<th>Air pollution savings</th>
</tr>

<td><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_recycling" title="Aluminium recycling">Aluminium</a></td>

<td>&#160;—</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ferrous_metal_recycling" title="Ferrous metal recycling">Steel</a></td>
